B005BJ7Y54|5.0|The name of this San Francisco band pretty much consolidates every traditional pop song down to its root cause.<br /><br />The band takes pop roots and branches them out, sometimes elevating them to heavy-metal heights and acid-rock lengths, as on the sprawling \\"Die,\\" which contains the lines, giddily voiced, \\"None of us is gonna be just fine / No, we're all going straight to hell tonight.\\"<br /><br />At other times, band mates Christopher Owens and JR White (with three new members added for Girls' second release) settle into hypnotic, lo-fi splendor, as on the sunny, yet shady \\"Alex,\\" with lead singer Owens mumbling his lines in a drug-addled manner. Or the down-tempo, blues-rocker \\"How Can I Say I Love You,\\" with its bittersweet message, \\"How can I say I need you? ... how can I say I want you? ... now that you've said everything I said to you / To somebody new?\\"<br /><br />To be sure, \\"Father, Son, Holy Ghost,\\" so named to illustrate the spiritual kinship Girls feels toward music, employs a variety of moods.<br /><br />The album kicks off with the psych-surf \\"Honey Bunny,\\" bouncy and strange like Syd Barrett-era Pink Floyd. The roiling, rollicking \\"Vomit\\" moves from ominous to dewy-eyed in the space of a few minutes. The bulk of the songs are backed by a three-piece gospel choir, a key ingredient that unifies the frantic mix.<br /><br />Toward the end is the album's shining highlight, \\"Forgiveness,\\" a slow-cooker about letting go of deep grudges before they tear you apart, with its woozy keys, crisp acoustic guitar and bellowing bass drum. A bleary Owens croaks, \\"No one's gonna find any answers / If you're just trying to hide / From the things you know inside / Are the truth,\\" the eight-minute spectacle eventually boiling over into a fury of white-hot licks and percussive thunder.<br /><br />There is definitely something magic going on here (and not just the track called \\"Magic\\"). \\"Holy Ghost\\" is an adventure inside a rock 'n' roll Bizarro World. It feels as if this is music that's been put to wax before -- there's that deja-vu tingle -- but the riffs and melodies are slightly off balance, like a chair at the local coffee shop. The tightly wound, gritty, almost claustrophobic sensation that permeates \\"Holy Ghost\\" comes from Girls' intentional choice to record it in a small, concrete studio in the heart of the Tenderloin.<br /><br />That decision in itself may be a bit of nostalgia for Owens, a one-time member of the Children of God (now scarily rebranded as the less conspicuous Family International), who lived on the streets of San Francisco for a time after leaving the religious cult at the age of 16. Owens is graced with an unsuspectingly powerful voice. It creeps along for a while, but by the fourth song, he opens up and takes charge. Owens is particularly affecting on \\"My Ma,\\" a feverish ode to his lost childhood, and a family with which he now must be completely at odds. His voice cracks with raw emotion as he sings, \\"Oh God, I'm tired / And my heart is broken / It's so hard to feel so all alone / And so far, so far away from home.\\"<br /><br />\\"Holy Ghost\\" is a weighty, existential affair. As such, it will never translate to the fresh-faced, overly overt, Day-Glo dance floor that is today's pop landscape. But for those who prefer meals, not snacks, \\"Holy Ghost\\" is the kind of album that gently gets under your skin and stays there. It's easily one of the most original releases of the year.re's that deja-vu tingle -- but the riffs and melodies are slightly off balance, like a chair at the local coffee shop. 0385534639|5.0|If there is magic, in the world as I know it, it is found in the pages of books.  And Erin Morgenstern's debut novel, The Night Circus, is the most magical, transporting experience I've had in quite some time.  It opens in 1873, backstage in a New York theater with these words, \\"The man billed as Prospero the Enchanter receives a fair amount of correspondence via the theater office, but this is the first envelope addressed to him that contains a suicide note, and it is also the first to arrive carefully pinned to the coat of a five-year-old girl.\\"  And so it is that Hector Bowen learns of the existence of his daughter, Celia.  Hector's response to his change in circumstances is unenthusiastic, but he soon discovers that Celia is a chip off the old block.  You see, Hector only pretends to be an illusionist on stage.  The magic he practices is entirely real--and Celia has inherited talent.<br /><br />Hector is not content to merely mentor his daughter.  He contacts his associate, Mr. A. H- about a wager.  A deal is struck, after which that gentleman returns to London and adopts a prot&eacute;g&eacute; of his own, the orphaned Marco.  These two young people are raised unconventionally, studying magic under their tutors' philosophies, all the while knowing there will be a competition between the two of them some day.  Enter impresario Chandresh Lef&egrave;vre, who has imagined something wondrous.  He explains:<br /><br />\\"More than a circus, really, like no circus anyone has ever seen.  Not a single large tent but a multitude of tents, each with a particular exhibition.  No elephants or clowns. No, something more refined than that.  Nothing commonplace.  This will be different, this will be an utterly unique experience, a feast for the senses.  Theatrics sans theater, an immersive entertainment.  We will destroy the presumptions and preconceived notions of what a circus is and make it something else entirely, something new.\\"<br /><br />And so the venue for the magical competition between Celia and Marco is born.  And there you have the bare bones of Ms. Morgenstern's story.  There's no need to tell more.  The magic of this tale is in the language and the characters and in Ms. Morgenstern's infinitely rich, romantic imagination.<br /><br />The circus, it is said, \\"arrives without warning.\\"  Not so this novel.  There have been all kinds of pre-pub buzz and hype for this title.  There's a reason; this book is so special that almost all who read it take note.  Ms. Morgenstern's tale transcended the page and brought true enchantment to my oh-so-ordinary life. As it happens, one of the characters could be speaking for the author herself:<br /><br />\\"I find I think of myself not as a writer so much as someone who provides a gateway, a tangential route for readers to reach the circus.  To visit the circus again, if only in their minds, when they are unable to attend it physically.  I relay it through printed words on crumpled newsprint, words they can read again and again, returning to the circus whenever they wish, regardless of time of day or physical location.  Transporting them at will.<br /><br />When put that way, it sounds rather like magic, doesn't it?\\"<br /><br />Oh yes, it does!  And I expect to be returning to this circus for many years to come.rn.  And there you have the bare bones of Ms. Morgenstern's story.  There's no need to tell more. B000088DSQ|5.0|The Harnoncourt Teldec recordings are most well-engineered recordings of classical music I have ever heard, and this set is my favorite among them. The third piano concerto in particular was never my favorite work, but I cannot stop listening to this particular recording of it. Something about the combination of Harnoncourt's style and the Teldec engineering brings incredible power to the sound of the strings that I have not heard anywhere else. I also love the position of the piano in the mix, which gives you the impression that you are in the hall rather than listening to a recording. The piano is too far forward in too many concerto recordings. Even though the piano is never right in your face, there is no lack of detail; you can clearly hear Aimard breathing on the cadenza of the first movement of the third, which adds to the tension of the moment. The whole set makes you feel more purely connected to the performances than any other classical recording I know of. Great stuff.

There are a few riffs during some of the songs that grab your attention.  But, there are no hooks to keep your interest.  Just some talking over a jam session.    There is a live version of \\"Those Damned Blue Collared Tweekers\\" at the end.  I guess that is the album's only saving grace for me.

185984054X|4.0|Agnes Gonxha Bojaxhiu, commonly known as Mother Teresa,  is widely regarded as a saint, a salutation which the Catholic Church is sure to make official.  Hitchens questions this common acceptance, looking at what she actually did rather than how she has been portrayed.  Even using the altruist-collectivist ethical yardstick (or meter stick?) that he does, Mother Teresa doesn't fare well.<br /><br />Hitchens holds that Mother Teresa is a dogmatic cultist who believes that suffering and poverty are spiritual goods, (who once referred to excrutiating suffering as being \\"kissed by Jesus\\" --since one has the opportunity to \\"share\\" in his suffering) and is fundamentally only interested in furthering the theological agenda of the Church.  As such, she keeps herself and all those around her poor and suffering.  Her Missionaries of Charity organization, while having some 600 missions in more than 100 countries, is poor by choice (having once even rejected a building because an elevator for the handicapped was legally required to be installed, something she considered too extravagant -- apparently it interfered with her missions' ability to carry the poor up and down the flights).  These 'missions' also have been reported to reuse IV needles, keep orphans in the most deplorable conditions, and withhold all but the most ineffective palliatives for the sick and dying.  Believing that it is \\"very beautiful for the poor to accept their lot,\\" she apparently kept things very beautiful.<br /><br />Apparently all of this occured unnecessarily.  Mother Teresa has accepted untold numbers of awards and countless donations (100's of millions, billions, who knows), achieving a cult-like status that could have easily provided outstanding services to the poor; however, she deliberately chose poverty and suffering (afterall, if they were no longer poor and suffering, what good would that do when \\"...the world is being much helped by the suffering of the poor people.\\" -MT)  Moreover, she has accepted this untold wealth from the most horrendous dictators and corrupt individuals, thereby lending her 'status' to assuage their guilt and improve their status.<br /><br />Finally, Hitchens takes umbrage with the stance of this \\"old virgin\\" and her fundamentalist opposition to any form of birth control that would empower women--structural reforms he believes will alleviate a great deal of suffering , suffering he regards as unecessary-- which makes her no friend to the poor.<br /><br />In a style that is Christopher Hitchens, this book is well researched and an effective polemic against a widely-regarded 'saint'.  This book is a good reminder that we need to apply rational scrutiny even to those most widely regarded amoung us.accepted this untold wealth from the most horrendous dictators and corrupt individuals, thereby lending her 'status' to assuage their guilt and improve their status.<br /><br />Finally, Hitchens takes umbrage with the stance of this \\"old virgin\\" and her fundamentalist opposition to any form of birth control that would empower women--structural reforms he believes will alleviate a great deal of suffering , suffering he regards as unecessary-- which makes her no friend to the poor.<br /><br />In a style that is Christopher Hitchens, this book is well researched and an effective polemic against a widely-regarded 'saint'. As more children are sent off to die each year in front of the entire nation, the people of Panem are growing increasingly hostile towards their government and whispers of change are going around. B002VL3R1K|3.0|Sometimes I just get the idea into my mind to read totalitarian ideology.  Just like dystopian literature it can be enlightening to put your worldview up against those which flatly reject what one's society puts forth.  After briskly reading through the Communist Manifesto I decided to tackle a work which had held my interest for some time.  The work of course was Mein Kampf, the semi-autobiography/propaganda work by one of history's justifiably infamous despots.    Actually the book was at times mental torture.  It is long, tedious in places, and reading over too much of its content in one afternoon could get my head literally aching.  That said the historical fascination drove me onwards.  Although rather weary at the end I am rather glad I took the time to read it.    Hitler discusses his early life, his failure as an artist, the shaping of his ideology, a few of his World War I experiences, and the rise of the Nazi party preceeding the failed coup at Munich.  As the forward and page notes point out however, not all of this information is conveyed accurately by the author (big surprise).    The book does not lay out a clear and carefully laid out explanation of Nazi ideology.  This is not surprising.  Hitler was not yet in power.  His ideas may not have fully solidified if they ever did.  Nazism like most large movements was far from monolithic but instead composed of the ideas of individuals from sometimes hostile viewpoints. There are some disturbing hints of things to come however.  Hitler's racism and anti-semitism are raw, paranoid, and clearly set forth.  So is a perverse utilitarian concept which would culminate in the forced sterilization or murder of the mentally handicapped.  The enmity towards France and the goal of expanding German influence in the east present forshadows of the wars which were to come.  Hitler's religious ideology remains rather an enigma.  He does make a number of references to religion in his book but as Abraham Foxman points out in the introduction there is evidence Hitler was privately opposed to Christian belief.  Even from the public veneer presented in Mein Kampf it seems clear that his religious beliefs (if he had them) were not what was really motivating him.  Apart from anti-semitism, pro-German racism, and a somewhat more justified fear of Marxism, another strain of thought runs through the work.  This was a particularly ruthless strain of Social Darwinism.  Hitler clearly had a predatory mind which was extended to the worth of human beings.  Hitler may have been insane; I do not know for certain.  He was clearly evil though.    So what can I say in summation.  Should I recommend this book?  That depends.  If you have his sorts of tendencies, please do not read this book to reinforce them.  If you are simply interested in history, Mein Kampf might be worth your time but remember that it is how Hitler wanted to present himself and is not entirely accurate.  This book might be a pain to struggle through.  You might find it disturbing.  Hopefully you will.  Nazism really was a revolutionary ideology but in nearly all the wrong ways.  Just like totalitarian communism the result was the deaths of millions.  This book is fascinating, informative, tedious, and frankly presents very evil ideas.  It can and has influenced people in very terrible ways.    If you choose to read do so at your own caution.

0307887898|5.0|Eric Ries defines a startup as \\"a human institution designed to create new products and services under conditions of extreme uncertainty.\\"  As a new college professor -- trying to serve student learning for the first time in a new environment -- I find that the principles of his innovative practices extend far beyond the boardroom or garage.  Build, measure, learn, and then pivot (or persevere): I build all the time: class plans, syllabi, assignments. 0307887898|5.0|An an entrepreneur and angel investor with several startups under my belt, I believe that Eric Ries' The Lean Startup is a MUST READ for anyone involved in startups these days.  If I had to recommend one book to fledgeling entrepreneurs in today's market, it would be this book - no question.    People working in large and established companies, however, might need this book even more.  The methodology described in \\"The Lean Startup\\" just might be the antidote to \\"The Innovator's Dilemma\\" that all established and successful companies must deal with.    Ries defines a startup as: \\"... a human institution designed to create a new product or service under conditions of extreme uncertainty.\\"  Large companies are full of such \\"human institutions\\" and when it comes to creating new products they are dealing with the same conditions of uncertainty as startups.  If anything, large companies are much more likely to commit larger and more costly mistakes than startups and much slower to react to them.    If you are an entrepreneur you need to study and practice the advice in this book.  If you want to be an entrepreneur and start a company, but you believe that you need a ton of VC money to do so, \\"The Lean Startup\\" will set you straight and motivate you.    But don't let the title mislead you; people working in organizations that are as far removed from the typical image of a startup as can be (e.g. large companies, government organizations) need to read, absorb and practice the lessons in this book even more to remain competitive.

B004J4XGN6|5.0|The title may seem over the top, but actually the implications of Eric's new book are huge. I can't remember a text in recent years that predicted such significant conclusions for society, education, and world business.    **Concepts in the book that will make your head spin:    1) Ries's Law (I'm coining this right now) - Moore's Law that states the cost of new technology will rapidly decrease thereby reducing technological risk of innovation. It's not until The Lean Startup that we realize the cost of reaching customers and thereby market risk will also rapidly decrease.    2) Entrepreneur mythology as we know it is wrong (aka Steve Jobs is not what he seems). Iconic innovators ala the founders of Apple are believed by the public to be successful because of their creative vision of the future. The Lean Startup shows that in order for founders to be successful, they must test each element of there vision against reality.    3) Demystifying startup metrics. Even in a startup mecca like NYC, few entrepreneurs understand the metrics that drive their business. Eric's Innovation Accounting paradigm will change the early-stage VC industry as we know it.      **What I like about the book:    1) Comprehensive and a pleasure to read.    2) Real world examples (case studies) from tech startups to the non-profit industry. You will not find anything like these in other books.    3) Case studies in chapters 6 & 7 alone are worth the price of the whole book.      **What I don't like:    1) The chapter titles are a bit confusing and non-descriptive.    2) I could see some savvy entrepreneurs getting bored with the beginning when there's really great content in the later chapters. But if you're new to Lean Startup, the beginning chapters are a perfect introduction for you.    3) Some of the sources are outdates. I recently checked the links at the back of the book and gotten a couple 404's, but after searching on Google was able to find the sources. (this might be fixed now, not sure)      **Final Words  As someone who is very active on the latest research into innovation and entrepreneurship as well as an long-time entrepreneur myself, I love Eric's book and think that it's going to become a true classic and stand out as THE business book to read in 2011-2012. Don't listen to the haters, just look at the reviews from people like Marc Adreesen (founder of Netscape) and Dustin Moskovitz (cofounder of Facebook), these people don't endorse you unless you you are the real deal. Eric & The Lean Startup are the real deal.

B005J7K964|5.0|I used to possess the laserdisc release of Moroder's Metropolis, and have kicked myself for many years for ever parting with it.  I replaced it with a Japanese import laserdisc which documented the pathetic state of preservation the film was in before Moroder did his restoration.  Moroder found and incorporated fragments of footage, including scenes from the opening pleasure garden and the still of the original Maria's tomb monument, adding tantalizing perspective and characterization to what had survived as a bombastic allegory.  I was thrilled to hear of the eventual discovery of nearly all the missing footage in Argentina, and even more thrilled when the fully restored film appeared on blu-ray last year.  If you don't have that one, buy it in preference to the Moroder.  But if you have the full restoration, then why not get the Moroder, too?  It documents an important phase in the eventual reincarnation of this film.<br />What about the score?  It is a futuristic 1920's film, and the 1980's music seems to work with it somehow.  During the \\"silent\\" era live music accompanied projection of the film, so there was great flexibility in sound.  In large theaters, an organ or orchestra would accompany the projection, while in small theaters there would be a piano or small organ.  Now, nearly a century later, home video releases have all kinds of newly recorded soundtracks, from piano to organ to orchestra.  Some releases provide alternative soundtracks, so that one can choose from two or more options.  If one does not like any of the soundtracks, one can always turn the sound off and watch the film silently.  So, I welcome the release of Moroder's version, which is not the definitive Metropolis of all time, but was as close to definitive as one could get at the time.<br /><br />PS 21 March 2012.  Finally I have been able to compare the Moroder blu-ray with the Complete Metropolis blu-ray, both from Kino and both superlative.  The Moroder transfer is just as crisp on their shared material, and sometimes crisper.  The tinting occasionally obscures detail (faces of the children on their entrance scene) but mostly enhances the composition of the scene (stadium in brown with blue skies and white clouds above).  Moroder's use of conversational subtitles instead of intertitles helps the dramatic flow, and reduces the feeling of seeing a lecture instead of a film.  Moroder's musical score once in a while gets cloying, but respects the scene changes and moods more so than most silent scores.  One would never want to do without the extra footage presented on the Complete Metropolis, but Moroder's version is a serious piece of film rejuvenation, which proved that there was a public interested in the restoration of silent films.  Moroder made the Complete Metropolis feasible.ir shared material, and sometimes crisper. 0553582038|1.0|SPOILER ALERT: (but not much to spoil, really)  If I had to describe A Feast for Crows in one word it would be BORING. Like everyone else, I thought the first three books were magnificent, but this book took me longer to read than the first three combined, because it was so... boring.      I think everyone has discovered Martin's style by now.  The first part of his chapters are always slow, with lots of descriptions of clothing and banners and how somebody's chamber pot smells, then the last four pages of the chapter are exciting. You read the slow stuff because you know there will be a payoff at the end of the chapter. In Feast, there are very few payoffs, just more descriptions and more non-action.     When I was reading it, I found myself thinking about other things, and then would realize the chapter was done and I didn't remember anything I had read. Are we really supposed to care about the childhood memories of a character that we do not know, or the genealogy of some random family, or the confusing \\"fever dreams\\" (I love how Martin continuously uses that phrase, a not-so-subtle subliminal plug for one of his other books) of a character we don't really like? By the way, has anyone else noticed that there are a lot of people suffering from fevers in these books? I think every single main character has had a life threatening fever at some point in the series, with some characters having multiple fevers. Martin should have called this one \\"A Festival of Fevers\\".     Unlike most people, I like Cersei's and Brienne's stories, as I think they are interesting characters. So I should like this book, right? Wrong. I don't like it because even though half the book is about them, they don't do anything for the entire novel. Cersei just plots and hates while Brienne just wanders down the road to find... absolutely nothing. Then the cliffhanger with Brienne is she says some word?  That is the cliffhanger?      Sam's story could have been summed up in a few pages. You cango ahead and skip his chapters, because all that happens is he rides on a boat to Oldtown for the entire book. Are they attacked by the kraken that Martin mentioned was spotted in some earlier book? No! Does anything happen on this laboriously long and tedious journey? No! He hooks up with Gilly and the old man dies, but that's not enough to fill the many chapters devoted to him.  The old guy says that Danerys is the chosen one? Wow what a shocker. We could have learned that info in a much less painful way. If all the info in the Sam chapters was condensed to one chapter, it would still be uneventful and boring.     If Sam did little, Arya does even less. I'm not even sure what happened to her now that I am trying to remember.    Sansa--somehow, Martin has done even less with her than Sam and Arya. Sansa, literally, does absolutely nothing in this book. She sits in a castle. That is it.    The sea pirate people are so boring that I had to start skipping whole sections of pages to get to some dialog that might move the story along. Who are they and why should I care? They devoted a chapter to the girl who looked like she was going to take over the Seastone Chair, but oops, she doesn't. Why was it necessary to even have that chapter? Moreover, why was it necessary to have these people and their aimless, meandering story in the book at all? Does Martin think we don't have enough characters to keep up with already?    The chapters that really take the cake, however, are the utterly useless Dorne chapters. In what sort of vacuum does Martin write that he would think that anyone would care to read this? The chapters are already about characters no one has heard about in 3500 pages of reading, but then to top it off he makes these characters do... wait for it... nothing! There is a chapter about Arys the Kingsguard and when you read it, you're like, \\"I guess he's going to be a main character now and I should get to know his character.\\" But no! After all of ONE chapter being devoted to him, he gets his head cut off. Great. Glad I got to know him. Martin also introduces the plot to put Myrcella on the throne and I was like \\"Ok, this is where these chapters are going, that's why Martin is taking us here.\\" But no! The plot fails, and that's the end of the story. So what has happened? Absolutely N O T H I N G. The woman behind the plot is then a prisoner in a tower for twenty pages when nothing, and I mean nothing occurs to her. You really feel the tedium of her being locked in the tower as you are locked in reading dry page after dry page of her laying in her bed and then using her bedpan, going back to bed, and not having dialogue with anyone. For 20 pages. The \\"payoff\\" with this is that Dorne has secretly been plotting with the Targareans? Who gives a $#!%? I cannot muster any interest whatsoever for the Dorne story.    This disjointed, slow paced, unrelated-to-anything-else-in-the-series piece of trash is a disgrace to the series. Do yourself a favor and just read the Wikipedia summary. It will be a lot more exciting and you won't waste a lot of time reading about \\"fever dreams.\\" I don't blame Martin though; many writers are self indulgent. I blame his \\"editor\\" for letting him put his name on this lump of garbage.

B005974CDU|4.0|A Dramatic Turn of Events is not so much a return to form as it is a return to coherent song writing without abandoning the dynamic twists and world class musicianship that mark every Dream Theater album. The album is decidedly positive, even triumphant sounding especially when compared to the dark tone of the bands last two albums, Systematic Chaos and Black Clouds and Silver Linings. As with much of DT's work, ADTE borrows both thematic and musical elements from previous albums. But whereas SC and BSCL sounded stale and exhausted the bands latest work succeeds in creating something fresh and hopeful, perhaps a cross between the best elements of Octavarium and the the first disc of 6 degree's. In fact this is the bands most focused effort, by far, since Train of Thought albeit coming from a completely different musical and personal direction.     What I like most about ADTE is the amount of restraint shown by both Petrucci and Rudess. Both players are phenomenal musicians with chops for days. However, more recent albums have featured overindulgent back and forth soloing (even for a prog band) that extended songs by minutes and oftentimes added little emotional punch to the material. Such restraint allows ADTE to breath and provides Labrie and Myung space to turn in some of their best work in years. While difficult to say, this change may be partially attributable to the departure of Mike Portnoy who took a decidedly heavy-handed, perhaps controlling approach with the band in recent years. Inter-band dynamics are tough to decipher but it's hard to argue with the bands less claustrophobic sound and the inclusion of more melodically interesting material sans Portnoy.    While I am loath to do a song by song review This is The Life, Bridges in the Sky, and Breaking All Illusions are initial bests with On the Backs of Angels and Lost Not Forgotten being my least favorite tracks. The reality is that there are no weak or bad songs on this album. Labrie has returned with asolid performance after wallowing in mediocrity while trying to cope with the bands generic metal sound structures on SC and BCSL. It's also great to see Myung with heavy influence and lyric writing credits. He bass work is always more under the surface but his tone and contribution is notably more significant and a treat for fans. Rudess sounds more creative and less apt to reintroduce keyboard parts and solos with ill-fitting, mood altering effects. I suppose I have to say something about Mike Mangini. His playing is solid and allows the material to breath compared to his predecessor. I think the change was good for the band mentally and challenged them to write a better album. B004P1YX6M|4.0|DG has struck gold in the past with young winners of the International Chopin Competition (Pollini, Argerich), not to mention a notorious non-winner, Ivo Pogorelich, before he lapsed into terminal eccentricity. Since it is held only every five years, the competition raises unusually high expectations, and the 2010 event drew exceptional playing (as heard on YouTube) form the young Russian woman who won, Yuliana Avdeeva. For some reason DG passed her up and chose to sign instead one of the two pianists who shred second prize, a 26-year-old Austrian. Not many German pianists have excelled in Chopin, to be sure, and there's a drought among outstanding German pianists to follow in the footsteps of Alfred Brendel. Did those considerations weigh heavily on the decision?<br /><br />DG also signed the 2005 winner, Rafal Blechacz, who made a sensational debut bu returned to continue his studies in philosophy - of all things - his specialty being aesthetics and the philosophy of music. Wunder won't turn out to be a flash in the pan, I venture. His recital begins with an extremely assured and poised reading of Sonata no. 3 in B minor. I think that buyers of Chopin CDs tend to fall into two groups, those who are content to buy Arthur Rubinstein's recordings, looking no further, and those at the opposite extreme, who have heard dozens of versions of every work. For the latter, there's a danger that familiarity will breed numbness. Yet another accomplished reading doesn't stir the blood, and on the other hand one's ears are pricked by the slightest hint of dullness or conformity. I've known connoisseurs to exchange a simple phrase - \\"the real thing\\" - to indicate that a new pianist has jumped over any number of invisible hurdles.<br /><br />In this case, I do think that Wunder (what a name, the equivalent of being named Miracle in English or being Stevie Wonder) is the real thing. There are no mannerisms,  stiffness, or competition whiz-kid tricks. He's sensitive and solid throughout the sonata. Touchstones like the lovely modulation in the middle of the Largo are handled effortlessly. None of this can be taught, and that's very good news. Is this a truly distinctive performance? Hard to say, because \\"sensitive and solid\\" aren't the same as deeply insightful or charismatic. My guess is that the charisma quotient will remain low with Wunder but that his musicality will keep maturing. He takes the finale with a measured tread, walking the line between solid and stolid, yet n the end my attention was held. The piano, by the way, sounds very good, and the pianist's technique seems to be assured without being dazzling. Happily, he never pounds and never tinkles, putting me in mind of Nelson Freire, who is the kind of artist Wunder might turn into one day.<br /><br />A more difficult test comes in the Polonaise-Fantasie  Op. 61, a longish work at 14 min. in Wunder's hands that requires the soloist to capture the elusive mood of the polonaise, a form invented by Chopin, as well as the ability to organize a work that is rhapsodic and spontaneous feeling. Horowitz made a specialty of mesmerizing audiences with this piece, but Wunder takes a very different road, eschewing high dramatic contrast in favor of inwardness and a flow of beautiful tone. There's a peculiarly graceful limp in the way that Chopin's polonaises and mazurkas move, and knowing this, many pianists exaggerate the lopsided phrasing in order to show that they catch on. Horowitz was a master of exaggeration (Virgil Thomson applied the phrase in the Forties as a detraction, but it needn't be) while Wunder aims to master understatement. He delivers a subdued but not boring reading, de-emphasizing the rhapsodic element.  He might have been better off keeping closer to the usual timing of 11 min., but even if the performance felt a little drawn out, it was highly acocmplished.<br /><br />Ballade no. 4 in F minor is a required work in the Chopin Competition, I believe, and Avdeeva, the winner, delivered a reading that started out a bit ordinarily but reached extraordinary depth and passion by the end - maybe it won her the gold medal. Wunder remains in the vein of his earlier readings, giving us mature musicality that isn't quite distinctive. The rising Argentine pianist Ingrid Fliter, also a second-prize winner, has a more individual voice, but then, she won in 2000 and has experience over Wunder. In this Ballade his chief failing is that he doesn't find a way to make the repetitions of the main theme build into a devastating conclusion; they tend to sound like repetitions only.<br /><br />finally, another required work, I believe, the Andante Spianato and Grande Polonaise Op. 22, and here for the first time i'm tempted to say \\"good Chopin, for a German.\\" This music is meant to be free and even abandoned in its onrush of melody, while Wunder is lyrical, correct, and controlled. One immediately wants to add that the musicality is always present and impressive. We live in an era of rising accomplishment in leading conservatories, and Wunder exemplifies the astonishing way that young musicians are able to absorb (mimic?) styles that evoke real sophistication. What they don't evoke is a strong personality or point of view. Going back to Yundi Li, who won the gold medal in 2000, I can't say that he is better in this music than Wunder, yet neither quite captures the magnetism, even if very soft passages, of Michelangeli, Pletnev, or Argerich when I turned to them for comparison.<br /><br />You can be the real thing, then, but the great thing still lies ahead of you.er, delivered a reading that started out a bit ordinarily but reached extraordinary depth and passion by the end - maybe it won her the gold medal. Wunder remains in the vein of his earlier readings, giving us mature musicality that isn't quite distinctive. The rising Argentine pianist Ingrid Fliter, also a second-prize winner, has a more individual voice, but then, she won in 2000 and has experience over Wunder. B005CAAURG|5.0|Queen's sixth album News of the World was released in November of 1977 in the States.<br />The album was recorded in the height of the punk rock movement in the UK at Wessex Studios with the band self-producing once again with Mike Stone engineering (last time Mike would work with the band as he went on to produce albums for Journey, Asia and Styx member Tommy Shaw among others).<br />Aside from the graphic album cover depicting a giant robot-like creature who killed the four Queen band members (thank drummer Roger Taylor for that concept) but also meaning it was a baby robot who injured the human like a baby injuring a bug and saying \\"what have I done\\"), there are nothing but great songs throughout.<br />The album opens with the one-two punch of the US #4 charting \\"We Will Rock You\\" and \\"We Are the Champions\\". The former was written by guitarist Brian May and is known for its stomp-stomp-clap beat and rally cry. The latter was frontman Freddie Mercury's victory cry which has become a victory song when sports teams would win championships. Next, is drummer Roger Taylor's answer to the punk movement \\"Sheer Heart Attack\\" (which was written for the album with the same title three years earlier but not finished until 1977). The song was as punk as Queen ever got (ironically The Sex Pistols were in the studio next door recording their only album when this track was recorded) and Roger plays drums, bass and rhythm guitar on this track. Brian's \\"All Dead, All Dead\\" is a soft beautiful ballad sung by Brian with Freddie on backing vocals. Bass player John Deacon's \\"Spread Your Wings\\" follows and is a mid-tempo rocker about one not giving up on his dreams. That track was released as a single in Europe and was the first single released where there were no backing vocals from the other Queen members. The first side closed with \\"Fight From The Inside\\" is another heavy rocker sung and written by Roger (whom also played guitar and bass on the track and did all the vocals on this track).<br />Side two began with the lyrically dirty \\"Get Down Make Love\\" and is one of the few blatant sexual anthems that Freddie wrote with raunchy guitar playing by Brian (lets see Britney Spears, Katy Perry, Lady Gaga, Christina Aguilera and/or Beyonce sing (let alone attempt to write) something like that). Brian steps out in front on \\"Sleeping On The Sidewalk\\" which is a bluesy shuffle track with some great guitar work and was recorded without overdubs (save Brian's harmony vocals). John's \\"Who Needs You\\" is another great track with Freddie's elegant vocals and featuring Brian and John on acoustic guitars. Brian's \\"It's Late\\" is almost in the same vein as some of the epics they wrote in the past, but alot heavier and not strange. The ending is great as Roger goes into this insane Bonham/Peart like drumming, if not better. It was released as a heavily edited single in the US and flopped but the song rocks. The lyrically depressing but superb \\"My Melancholy Blues\\" is an all-out jazz song featuring Freddie's sorrowful vocals which is a great song for those whom have had either broken hearts or bad days and ends the album in a relaxing fashion (and like \\"Spread Your Wings\\" has no backing vocals on it).<br />News Of the World peaked at #3 in the US and sold over 4 million in the US alone to date because of the songs and not the personalities in the band and News of the World became Queen's best American selling album until The Game in 1980 tied this.<br />In September of 2011, Queen re-releases this classic masterpiece as well as the next four studio albums (Jazz, The Game, Flash Gordon Soundtrack and Hot Space) as all newly remastered as 2-CD Deluxe Editions painstakingly remastered by Bob Ludwig (who did a remaster of A Night at the Opera for its 30th Anniversary in 2005) for most of the world (the outside US release was in July) and these new remasters buries any previous CD version of the back catalog.<br />The bonus CD for News Of The World contains a nice outtake of the fabled \\"Feelings Feelings\\" which was recorded for News but scrapped. Then you get BBC versions of \\"Spread Your Wings\\" and \\"My Melancholy Blues\\" done a month before News Of The World's release. Then you get a live recording of \\"Sheer Heart Attack\\" recorded in Paris in 1979 (different from the Live Killers take). Then a live version of the fast version of \\"We Will Rock You\\" recorded in Japan in 1982. The booklet that comes with this has just about everything that came with the original LP artwork, lyrics and credits plus extra photos of the band.<br />Some will say why buy again but I say go for it because of the remastering!<br />This album is highly recommended!elings Feelings\\" which was recorded for News but scrapped. Then you get BBC versions of \\"Spread Your Wings\\" and \\"My Melancholy Blues\\" done a month before News Of The World's release. Then you get a live recording of \\"Sheer Heart Attack\\" recorded in Paris in 1979 (different from the Live Killers take). Then a live version of the fast version of \\"We Will Rock You\\" recorded in Japan in 1982. The booklet that comes with this has just about everything that came with the original LP artwork, lyrics and credits plus extra photos of the band.   Some will say why buy again but I say go for it because of the remastering!   This album is highly recommended!
B005CAAU76|5.0|Queen's seventh album entitled Jazz was released in November of 1978.<br />The album has always had the distinction to many of Queen's fans of being Queen's worst 1970s album but I think it is great (as I found out when I first got the CD in April of 1992).<br />The album saw the band co-producing with Roy Thomas Baker for the first time since 1975's A Night at the Opera (they would not ever work together again on original material) and was recorded at Mountain Studios in Montreux, Switzerland and Superbear Studios in France with the band avoiding the British tax man.<br />We begin with lead singer Freddie Mercury's \\"Mustapha\\" which is a Middle Eastern sounding track and a unique opener. Freddie singing in Arabic is a grand way to kick off a diverse Queen record. Next was the album's US Top 30 hit \\"Fat Bottomed Girls\\" and penned by guitarist Brian May and is a great rocker dedicated to Brian, Roger and John's wives (or in Roger's case girlfriend) and was a concert staple on the 1978/1980/1982 US tours. Freddie's ballad \\"Jealousy\\" follows and is a beautiful song which did come out as a single in the States but flopped. On this remaster, the bass drum which was muted was restored and brought up in the mix. Next is the album's biggest US Top 30 hit \\"Bicycle Race\\" (which was the A-side to Fat Bottomed Girls) and this Freddie penned number was written whilst watching the Tour de France from his hotel suite. This song showed Queen did not ever take themselves too seriously. Next is bass player John Deacon's rocker \\"If You Can't Beat Them\\" which was John's first true rock number he wrote for the band. The first half ends with Freddie's homage to the excessive rock lifestyle called \\"Let Me Entertain You\\" which is a great rocker and was a staple live on the band's 1978/79 and 1980/81 tours.<br />We begin the second half with a May rocker called \\"Dead on Time\\" which is one of his best rockers. Next is a Deacon ballad \\"In Only Seven Days\\" about meeting someone and falling in love while on vacation (also one of few Queen tracks not to have backing vocal harmonies). The track would not have sounded out of place on either Billy Joel's The Stranger or 52nd Street albums as it has the feel of a Billy Joel tune musically. Next is Brian's jazzy \\"Dreamers Ball\\" which is a song which may sound lazy on studio version but when played live on the Jazz tour was better. Next is drummer Roger Taylor's \\"Fun It\\" with Freddie and Roger trading lead vocals. The rock-disco track sounds like a pre-cursor to Another One Bites the Dust on the next album. Next is Brian's \\"Leaving Home Ain't Easy\\" and has Brian May on all vocals and is a nice, acoustic number. The big UK hit and song that stalled at #86 in the US \\"Don't Stop Me Now\\" is next and one of Freddie's classic pop rock songs and a great song. We end the album with Roger's rocker \\"More of That Jazz\\" which had Roger on EVERYTHING (including all vocals, drums, guitars and bass) and is a great song. Before the song ends, the abrupt appearance of collage 'soundbites' of most of the tracks over the course of the album, probably spliced in to give the false sense of sounding like a commercial for Queen's Jazz album which Elektra Records probably did for radio promotion in 1978.<br />Jazz when released hit #6 in the US and went Platinum immediately but sales were soft compared to the four previous albums but still a great record nevertheless.<br />In September of 2011, Queen re-released this classic masterpiece as well as the other four studio albums from 1977 to 1982 (News Of The World, The Game, Flash Gordon Soundtrack and Hot Space) as all newly remastered 2-CD Deluxe Editions painstakingly remastered by Bob Ludwig (who did a remaster of A Night at the Opera for its 30th Anniversary in 2005) for most of the world (the worldwide release outside the US and Canada happened in June) and these new remasters buries any previous CD version of the back catalog.<br />The bonus CD for Jazz contains the single mix of \\"Fat Bottomed Girls\\" which is commonly on all greatest hits collections. Then you get an instrumental mix of \\"Bicycle Race\\". Next is \\"Don't Stop Me Now\\" with guitars that were not on the original mix. Then you get a live recording of \\"Let Me Entertain You\\" recorded in Montreal in 1981 (appears on Queen Rock Montreal). Then an early take of \\"Dreamers Ball\\". The booklet that comes with this has just about everything that came with the original LP artwork, lyrics and credits plus extra photos of the band.<br />Some will say why buy again but I say go for it because of the remastering!<br />This album is highly recommended!<br />Recommended!hich is commonly on all greatest hits collections. B003M98792|4.0|I'll never forget when I first watched Shogun Assassin. When I worked at a movie store, there was a regular customer who was always asking me about samurai and martial arts films. One day he came in with his own copy of Shogun Assassin, saying that I had to watch it since it was one of his all-time favorites. I watched it that same day and have never looked at samurai movies the same ever since. While Shogun Assassin is usually considered 'just' a B-film with over the top gore, I was mesmerized by the swordsmanship of Tomisaburo Wakayama. He made everything look so effortless, and if I didn't know any better, I'd have guessed that he was actually killing people! Eventually I learned that Shogun Assassin was actually the first two films in the Lone Wolf and Cub movie series but edited to move things along faster, and with a more modern soundtrack. Of course, I ended up watching all 6 Lone Wolf and Cub movies, though only the first three won me over. A lot of hardcore fans hate Shogun Assassin for 'ruining' Lone Wolf and Cub, but I don't think they understand that if it weren't for Shogun Assassin, a lot of people wouldn't have even known about Lone Wolf and Cub in the first place, outside of Japan at least.    Ogami Itto is the Shogun's executioner, and a master swordsman. He quits after being tired of the corrupt Shogun, and as a result, the Shogun sends a group of ninjas and kills Itto's wife. His infant son, Daigoro, is then given the choice of joining his mother, or going along with Ogami on the path of vengeance. Ogami sits in front of Daigoro with a ball and his sword, and whichever Daigoro goes for will determine his fate. Well, you know how cool shiny objects are to kids...Daigoro goes for the sword, and everything is set in stone. Ogami is ordered to commit seppuku before the Shogun's men, but defies them and takes them all out with the greatest of ease. He manages to construct a baby stroller for Daigoro as well, full of all kinds of weapons that will helpthem both out against their enemies. This is all just the beginning of a long blood bath.    To be honest, the movie is pretty much just a collection of Ogami Itto slicing and dicing bad guys [and girls], with very little emphasis on the plot. Since it's a cut up version of two movies, cramming the plot from both of those would've been hard to do, so they went with most of the action scenes, with an added narration by Daigoro which I actually like. The kills are over the top, with heads being split in half, limbs flying all over the place, and a neck-cutting scene that makes most people cringe. As I said earlier, Wakayama is incredible to watch, especially given that he's a little on the heavy side, but doing front flips like it's nobody's business and swinging his dotanuki sword as fast as say, anyone who's every played Zatoichi. The baby cart is a star in itself, with knives, spears and more being concealed within the handle and sides, though there's an even cooler weapon that isn't featured here, and you'll have to watch the Lone Wolf movies to see it in action. There's something here for everyone, and Shogun Assassin is essentially a sampler of the Lone Wolf movies. If you like what you see here, but want plot too, go watch the Lone Wolf movies or read the comic series. If there's anything I have to dock points for it's that they could've edited a few scenes better. That strangely long scene of Itto cutting into a guy's head as the hat and head split apart very, very, very, very slowly could've been trimmed. 'same with the scene where he's ambushed by ninjas in the woods. That scene always bugged me for some reason.    The picture and audio quality of the blu-ray are probably as good as we're going to get, unless they somehow have the team that restored The Wizard of Oz work on it. The picture is sharp and crisp, but there's still a lot of grain, lines and other things. It definitely looks better than any of the dvd releases that've come out. You can even see this for yourself in the special features where they compare this to a bootleg and the official dvd. As far as the audio goes, it gets the same great treatment, though it's a Linear PCM 2.0 mix. There is no Japanese audio option, just the English dub. Some people have complained about this but I don't understand why as this is Shogun Assassin...not the original movies it's cut from.    The special features are solid, with stuff for hardcore and casual fans alike. You get two commentary tracks: one with Ric Meyers and Steve Wilson and one with Davis Weisman, Jim Evans and Gibran Evans. The first track with Ric and Steve covers the sword fights, martial arts and Japanese tradition, and is very informative for those wondering about some techniques used in the movie. I'd like to say that Ric redeems himself here, after the embarrassment in the Kung Fu Hustle dvd interview he did a few years back. The other track is more about editing the movie to make it accessible for everyone, and how it came to be. It was cool to hear from Gibran, who voiced Daigoro when he was just seven. Next is an interview with Samuel L. Jackson, and while it's a laid back interview, I don't get why they chose to interview him for this, as all he does is talk about how much he loves Shogun Assassin, and the theater he used to go to as a kid that would always play cheap martial arts movies. I'll give him some credit though for pointing out a few things that viewers might not notice, like how Itto gave Daigoro a certain look in the well scene to figure out how deep it was and how much danger Daigoro was in. There are also program notes about the HD remastering, and a cool Restoration Gallery where you can see the improvement in picture quality from the old dvd and bootlegs compared to this blu-ray. Finally is the infamous trailer that most people have seen.    Overall the blu-ray is definitely worth picking up. While I do like the first three Lone Wolf movies better, for their plot and the less datedsoundtrack (the music here reminded me of Halloween 3: Season of the Witch's soundtrack), there's no denying that Shogun Assassin gets the job done. It was actually hard to review this since most people on this page have probably already seen the movie, and know what they're getting into. But for those who've never seen the movie, I think you'll be in for quite a treat.
0756405890|4.0|The enjoyment of books is more complex than it might seem at first. There are books that are awful. They have cliched characters, shallow plots and poor writing. These are book that are never going to be enjoyable. There are other books where you have to be in the right frame of mind to enjoy the book. The first time I read Umberto Eco's [[ASIN:015603297X Foucault's Pendulum]] I didn't like the book. I read it a few years later and loved it.<br /><br />The first time I read The Name of the Wind I checked it out of the library. I gave the book three stars and this is what I wrote:<br /><br />After the first 20 pages I almost put the book down.  But then I read on.  The characters had some depth and the story seemed interesting.  But the story went on and on and the main character kept acting in a foolish manner.  After 600 pages I just didn't care enough to read the next book.<br /><br />Amazon had the book on sale for the Kindle Reader and I bought a copy. Reading it again, I really liked the book.<br /><br />Patrick Rothfuss is a good writer. I find his characters engaging and the plot is interesting. As I wrote in the first review, the central character, Kvothe, does indeed to a lot of stupid things. In fact, he seems to specialize in not thinking about consequences. On the other hand, he's very young. He is also brilliant and talented and is very aware of the fact. With this reading, Kvothe's immaturity didn't bother me as much.  I upped my rating from three stars to four.<br /><br />Why not five stars? If Rothfuss where not such a good writer, the plot could be described as plodding. The Name of the Wind is an engaging book, but the span of Kvothe's life that the book covers is relatively small. B0025UHKNS|4.0|To start with, the 405cx can sync with Garmin's site through a bluetooth adapter, so I just need to go near the computer, and it will sync automatically. Once the info is on the Garmin site, I can replay my workout looking at speed, heart rate and elevation. I can relive the pain in great detail that way. Because the Garmin site keeps all of the GPS information, you can save routes to use like a standard 'course.' I've baselined a couple and run 'against' them.<br /><br />One of the features on the watch I use frequently is called 'Virtual Partner.' Basically you set a pace, and the watch will roll through a page showing you how far ahead of (or behind) that pace you are. Another feature I find helpful is the 'auto lap,' which allows me to create a lap every so many miles or minutes. It's interesting to see how the run progresses over time in terms of effort.<br /><br />I can also set up to three custom 'pages,' each with different types of information on it. For example, I have two: one with data for the overall run (time, average pace and distance), and then one for the current lap (average pace and time). The HRM page is configurable, too, so I've added the percentage as well as the actual. You can always have an HRM page. Once I start the workout, all I have to do to switch between the pages is touch the watch's bezel. There is also and 'auto scroll' mode, so you don't have to touch the watch.<br /><br />The watch has two modes--running and biking--and remembers the setting for each mode. That way, I can have one auto lap setting for running (1m, say) and another for biking (10m, e.g.).<br /><br />I'm not sure I would use this in a triathlon because switching between running and biking mode takes a little effort.<br /><br />There are some items that 'take some getting used to.' Garmin will tell you the touch bezel is easy to use, and it does allow for easier access to menus and features, but it requires a little practice to get right...definitely not something Iwould experiment with during the run. Also sweat can make the bezel inop, so I usually lock the watch in 'auto scroll' mode and go.<br /><br />One 'feature' I don't care for is that it's not possible to delete a workout. If you inadvertently start the workout, which is easy to do with the bezel locked, that 2.63s workout is there for-freaking-ever. You can generally avoid this if you're careful.<br /><br />The watch holds a charge pretty well. I can get about 6 to 8 hours of GPS-enabled workout per charge, and the watch only required about an hour or maybe two to charge completely. It's a little tricky, because you have to plan a little--I've started out only to find I had limited battery left. On one run, the watch just died, so I didn't get any of my precious data.<br /><br />Overall I'd give this watch 4 or 4.5 stars (out of 5). I picked it up at Amazon for a song (Gold Box) because their latest version is the 410, and they still had an inventory of these watches. I'm perfectly willing to be a rev back.<br /><br />As an alternative, the Garmin 110 is much, much simpler. I recently bought the 110 for my wife. If you just want to strap on the watch and get your speed, time and heart rate, the 110 is less expensive, and for the luddites out there, much less frustrating. The 110 is strictly a running watch, and doesn't have the Virtual Partner, but can upload to the Garmin site, so it's possible to analyze afterward if desired.<br />I've been using the watch for three months, so I probably have 50 to 60 workouts in that time, and would definitely recommend the watch.

0307887960|1.0|Take it from an Exercise Physiologist, you can't trick your body into burning fat (nor do you need to). If you severely restrict your body of carbohydrates, you WILL lose weight in a rapid fashion. Only problem is, it WON'T BE FAT WEIGHT that you are rapidly losing. If you value your muscle mass and the energy and fluid content that makes your muscles strong and gives them endurance, low carb is not the way to go. If you value an active, healthy metabolism, low carb is not the way to go. The reason millions of people have bought into the idea of low carb diets is the fact that you do get rapid weight loss. However, this is only because your body burns up muscle glycogen (stored sugar in the muscles that holds a large amount of water in the muscle cells)causing a rapid loss of weight (water) in the first several weeks on a low carb diet. Once this glycogen depletion is reached, the rapid \\"weight loss\\" ends, and a slowed metabolism is created. This is a survival response by the body to what it percieves as starvation, even if you are taking in plenty of calories from other food sources. Not only will you have less endurance for glycogen demanding exercise (endurance activities and high intensity activity), but you will have trouble losing any more \\"weight\\" as you pursue more fat loss. Once you give up on such a diet (which you will because it will stop \\"working\\") you will rapidly gain back the lost weight as your muscles replenish their glycogen reserves and fluid content (this is a good thing) allowing the metabolism to return to normal as it leaves what it percieved as a fight against starvation (the brain and nervous system preffer and require sugar). Also, any suggestion that the body's ability to turn protein into sugar makes up for not eating adequate carbohydrates is psuedoscience, and is not based on what is healthy, ideal, or fuctional for the body on a long-term basis. An active, exercising person will not be able to supply adequate sugar to feed an active metabolism by relying on the coversion of dietary protein into sugar within the body. Muscle tissue itself will begin to be broken down as this process is attempted (turning muscle protein into sugar), while all the while the muscles will become energy depleted lowering their ability to sustain calorie-burning exercise or an active metabolism.etabolism by relying on the coversion of dietary protein into sugar within the body. 0307887898|5.0|Eric Ries is the Peter Drucker of this century and his first book, The Lean Startup, lays the foundation for the future of entrepreneurship.  In it he elegantly summarizes what he has taught and learned in the crowd-sourced Lean Startup movement over the past 3 years:  startups are a discovery process and we entrepreneurs need a new management science that maximizes our probability of success.    There are a number of well-written general reviews here on Amazon so I'll substantiate my 5 star rating by picking one example.      A key principle of The Lean Startup is to organize operations in a way that accelerates the speed at which a startup can change the plan - or pivot - based on validated learning about the market.  Ries suggests having scheduled pivot-or-persist meetings where teams meet to review what they learned and decide whether to persist down the current path or pivot to a new direction.      While exceedingly simple in concept, this one tactic has transformed the chaotic nature of my startup operations into a systematic process where I'm making directed moves towards understanding what product to build for my market.      This is just one example but it is the type of benefit you can expect from The Lean Startup.  I expect CEOs everywhere to distribute copies to their teams.  Companies that don't adopt Lean Startup principles will lose to those that do.

They all have something you're not allowed or supposed to have but ultimately you find a way to want it and grasp it and if you're lucky keep it.<br /><br />Delirium portrays Lena as a young girl about to meet her life partner (I can't remember if there's a specific term used or not) and about to get her treatment against the delirium, love sickness.<br /><br />Every aspect of the plot was meshed together perfectly. I love that there was more to it then just the love story.<br /><br />Setting<br />Lena's world is a pretty brutal place. A required treatment to inhibit the ability to fall in love or feel anything remotely like love or hate is one of the ultimate free will abilities humans have.<br /><br />There's a citizen curfew and required badges or identification cards. Periodically there are raids to search homes.<br /><br />Writing<br />This was the first book by Lauren Oliver I've read. I did try to read Before I Fall but I just couldn't make my way through it. This book is written awesomely. I love that it was written from Lena's point of view. I love the detail that was given to her feelings and her surroundings.<br /><br />Originality<br />Again, love as a disease? Pure originality.<br /><br />Characters<br />Lena doesn't plan to fall in love or even rethink her governments plans for controlling their citizens. I like it when characters surprise themselves; learn more about themselves.<br /><br />Hana is Lena's best friend. She isn't too exposed as a character but we grasp her as the pretty, popular one.<br /><br />My awful secret is that I like to run with Hana partly because it's the single, sole, solitary shred of a thing that I can do better than she can, but I would never admit that out loud in a million years. - page 47 - ARC edition<br /><br />Lena and Hana run quite a bit together. This is one of the most important events of the entire story. Hana is also quite the rowdy one.<br /><br />Lena and Hana both meet Alex while exploring the back of one of the government buildings while on a late afternoon jog. He's a guard. But oh so much more. Alex's character is awesome.<br /><br />Romance<br />Lena and Alex are amazing together. They cover so much territory in such a short amount of time. Ninety-five days of one amazing love. One could almost be happy about that. Except, you can't because you want to hold on to that forever. But what are your options when treatment day comes?<br /><br />Cover/Title Bonus<br />The ARC cover is a bit too meek for my tastes but it fits fine. The title is absolutely perfect.<br /><br />The final cover does not appeal to me at all. I'd much prefer the ARC edition.<br /><br />Ending<br />One of the most touching endings I've ever read. I was so torn to see it end. I read this May 30 - 31, 2010. This doesn't even come out until February 1, 2011 and who knows when the next book will be out. I have so long to wait to see how this will continue.<br /><br />Series<br />The next two book titles are already on Goodreads.<br /><br />Pandemonium - publishing sometime in 2012<br /><br />Requiem - publishing sometime in 2013<br /><br />I highly recommend this to anyone who loves dystopias or rebellious teens. :)

B00006FDCJ|5.0|Never even imagined this could be done with a movie, I was blindsided     Wicked! The most encompassing multi-perspective pyschological experience I've ever had. David Webb Peoples adroitly exposes the cunning ways of evil, but it took a skillful director and powerful acting to keep the ridiculous Will Munny from being exposed for the completely selfish summation-of-all-stupidity he is. When watching \\"Unforgiven\\" be wise, be vigilant, and be wary of the company you keep or you get snakebit. This is no ordinary movie from the Hollywood mill; you must pay attention from the moment you see the solitary figure digging a grave under that tree until the moment the solitary figure returns in victory to stand over the grave under that stupid tree.  Now, if you get yourself snakebit, and your vision gets all blurred , and you don't know what you saw, look upon the Word to clear your vision, so you don't trip and fall into a grave.     This movie has been classified as a Cowboy Western because it has cowboys and they carry guns, and as such it is excellent. But, it's also a morality play ending tragically when the flawed hero dies. What is his blind spot? Where is the wisdom in this apparently nihilistic movie? David Webb Peoples has a wicked talent for wrapping a mystery within the facts presented upfront and repetitively. When the mysteries of this movie are solved every scene adds meaning. Even the character's names and the cursed guns Will Munny uses to kill reveal something. A major theme of \\"Unforgiven\\" is immorality and immoral uses of money serving a will-to-power, thus Will Munny. David Webb Peoples loads up his story with moral themes overflowing, and then demonstrates something essential about perception.       When it comes to physcological thrillers, the \\"The Crying Game\\" is a distant second to \\"Unforgiven.\\" The physcological effects on audiences are more insidious. Discombobulated critics babble on about deconstructing western mythology, reconstructionist history, yada yada yada... and totally miss the simple old fashion moral wisdom of this movie. Slipping through moral defenses at their weakest points, the author manages to overcome the judgment faculties of a viewer's mind more effectively than alcohol and lust. Once that's accomplished, he's able to mask the lying murdering no-good evil enemy, Will Munny, as the downtrodden vulnerable underdog protagonist, and mask the tragically flawed hero, Little Bill, as the fascist pig antagonist beating and kicking famous murderers who show up in his town to murder. This movie is almost too funny to be credible, but Peoples and Eastwood managed to keep their experiment from blowing up their careers. If you can't discern the good from the evil, than you're left with a blinding fog of nihilism, and you are completely oblivious to the fundamental and recurring theme, a crescendo rising to a prophetic blast from Gabriel's trumpet; good men are warped, weakened, and destroyed by evil associations.  The innocent either understand this movie or hate it for its creepy and subtle disorientating duplicity and misdirection: a funhouse affect. The way this movie digs itself into a viewer's mind to warp his perception reminds me of the \\"Ring;\\" really creepy and considering the social implications, \\"Unforgiven\\" reaches the apex of horror. Have you seen zombie flicks? Well, \\"Unforgiven\\" is a successful demonstration of mind warping control over the walking dead, who christians call unforgiven. Until EWTN showed me the way, I was one of the walking dead, recognizing stupid but blind to wisdom.      \\"Unforgiven\\" is as much of a comment on current day control of the masses by the various forms of media as it is a significant comment on moral ambiguity. The two subjects are related, because the enemy first appears at the open door, something always fills a vacuum, and nihilists place their faith in and seek answers from victorious strongmen who in turn look upon people as mere objects upon which they exercise and demonstrate their power. Never ridicule Nietzsche style self-willed ubermensches about the ultimate smallness of their power or \\"they will turn and tear you,\\" which is the full extent of their power.     Western expansion may have been driven by a desire to escape control by others, but run as far as they may, men carry the seeds of their destruction with them; reminds me of the Edgar Allan Poe story,\\"Masque of the Red Death.\\" \\"Unforgiven\\" is epic in its scope, and once understood the viewer finds many pearls of wisdom. I'm not going to write a book here, but I will refer you to the bible and the catechism of the catholic church; good vaccinations against evil.     The author concludes with a riddle concerning Mrs. Ansonia Feathers, that is worthy of a sphinx, in which you'll find the raison d'etre of the movie. We now know all too intimately. A fitting end to a funhouse ride. Clever.      Enjoy the experience and savor all the fine details of a rooting-tooting philosophical shoot-out, but caution: be wise, vigilant, and wary of who you ride with, partner. Happy trails.

Mike Senior has been writing the well-liked \\"Mix Rescue\\" column in Sound On Sound magazine since 2006, so he's an expert at taking a sometimes lackluster recording and crafting it into a mix that's something far greater than what it was before. With all of that experience comes a wealth of practical knowledge on mixing. Some of the many highlights of this book include his lengthy discussion of monitoring, mix preparation (the work you should do BEFORE you mix), how to achieve a balance in your mix using dynamics processing and EQ, and sweetening with effects. In conclusion, this is the book that I WISH I had when I first started recording and mixing. If you're new to mixing, get this book. If you're not new to mixing, get this book--you'll still learn a lot.

He spends some time developing the other competitors as characters (they are that) by describing their personalities, appearances, antics and their approach to memorization.  He also writes about overcoming some of the typical barriers that people encounter during any endeavour requiring extended periods of training but his description of the hurdles and his approach to successfully overcoming them makes his account interesting and worth reading.    Moonwalking with Einstein is not an instruction manual on how to do memory gymnastics but it does introduce the reader to the fact that these feats can be accomplished without any innate natural talent.  The people who are practicing memorization at this level are mostly utilizing techniques that take advantage of the minds natural strength at memorizing pictures while minimizing the need to retain digits and other items which it is less well suited to remember. The techniques for developing a super memory are presented but not taught in any detail by this book.      This book brings to light that the extreme memorization that is its subject, was no great feat in the past.  People in ancient times easily memorized things out of necessity and the current techniques are mostly reincarnations of methods used centuries ago.      This is a fun and interesting read.  I believe that when you have completed this book you will not only have been entertained by the authors clever writing but you will have also learned something of value.  I suggest this book for anyone.
